    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2011954532" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2011954532" />
    <a title="Travelin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21707944-travelin"><img alt="Travelin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1381132872p2/21707944.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2011954532?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Travelin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Travelin" href="/user/show/21707944-travelin">Travelin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2011954532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11589072759247794428">Lazy, simpleminded, almost unremmittingly tedious, although occasionally farcical, sometimes even funny, it's Hollywood action if it was written by a modestly sized Brit in longhand. Most of the casual racism exists in what's missing.<br/><br/>Before I quarter this book to keep the horses warm, a word about the author. You might want to turn away now, if you can't handle the truth. Many months ago I'd read half of some collection of his short stories and was left with the impression: moralist, trying too </span>
  <span id="freeText11589072759247794428" style="display:none">Lazy, simpleminded, almost unremmittingly tedious, although occasionally farcical, sometimes even funny, it's Hollywood action if it was written by a modestly sized Brit in longhand. Most of the casual racism exists in what's missing.<br /><br />Before I quarter this book to keep the horses warm, a word about the author. You might want to turn away now, if you can't handle the truth. Many months ago I'd read half of some collection of his short stories and was left with the impression: moralist, trying too hard, with too much rope to pull a light load. Then I read that one or more of his parents were published authors. Then I looked at his photo. Everything thus far screamed: prodigal son, overmatched by his legacy. <br /><br />To be fair, a lot of the action style writing, which I would normally consider "automatic writing" got noticeably better in the last 100 pages. It's probably hard to make such writing look effortless, since it far surpasses several books it sits athwart, written by Yanks, but firmly in the mystery or action genres. But given Kneale's background and Oxbridge education, we're forced to judge him on literary merits, if that's the mileu which wins him prizes.<br /><br />The tone of the book throughout is mild sarcasm regarding the way various white colonizers treated aboriginals. There are at least three problems with this. First, casual racism is delivered with such dry sarcasm, I begin to wonder if nonBrits might just miss the intended insults. I know a German woman who (rightfully) fails to catch indirect sarcasm such as this. Secondly, this dry version of racism is in fact understating the degree of racism that existed and probably continues to exist. Third, and most importantly, the aboriginals themselves are given only one voice, and also one so artificial that an author's note at the start of the book tries to apologize and back you into the aboriginal's version of English, but nothing can forgive such a tin ear.<br /><br />Mostly the aboriginals are just portayed as victims or maddened killers. This may be because the author's short list of acknowledgments seems to include no aboriginal names. This may be because virtually every character, aboriginal or Brit, is a cartoon which can be erased, slap sticked or played again and again in slightly different poses. <br /><br />But mostly it's just laziness. Given an almost foolproof framework, Kneale didn't spend the slightest amount of time studying Victorian geology, theology, Darwinism, racial theories, aboriginals, etc. He appears to have spent some time studying Manx dialect and learning quite a few naval terms.<br /><br />Aside from the glaring lack of unadorned aboriginal voices describing aboriginal traditions and insights, the book's casual racism lies in making one white man's rape of aboriginal women sound ultimately valuable. This insight is delivered by the one voice given to the aboriginals, as he is describing how he must teach his other half-breed breathren the white man ways. Pair this with the very last pages, which contain a contemporary grammar school teacher's report about one aboriginal child who was quite good at math tables, and the author begins to sound as simple-minded and condescending as his own eccentric British characters.<br /><br />In truth, it's hard to feel angry. First, I don't have the background. Second, this book seems to fall squarely into an utterly banal list of middling British books reprinted by Penguin. It's like a comment I saw once that British bankers are unfailingly "nice" face to face, although they are just as forgettable as the modern Penguin list of books, or, probably, its Victorian equivalent. I'm happy to report that "38 editions" probably refers to the number of translations and not the total number of print runs, at least in popularly read languages.<br /><br />I thought the only pleasure I would get from this book would be some kind of scathing review. But just having finished a far superior book, I'm finally reading for pleasure and not for the reviews they inspire. This book did push me to keep reading deeper into "The Bone People," although the Maori seem to be a more recently settled people, less thoroughly vanquished, with enough status for "The Bone People" to be treating the obverse of this book: a bizarre, naive white interloper whom locals can joke about having for dinner.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11589072759247794428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1344153506" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1344153506" />
    <a title="Ana Ovejero" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45220423-ana-ovejero"><img alt="Ana Ovejero" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602465865p2/45220423.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1344153506?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana Ovejero" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana Ovejero" href="/user/show/45220423-ana-ovejero">Ana Ovejero</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1344153506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6244069700840266583">This narrative is truly a historical adventure. The reader can feel the salt of the sea wind throughout the whole story along the humorous and dramatic moments lived by an expedition full of colourful characters.<br/><br/>It is the year 1857 and a voyage is leaving to Tasmania as it is believed to be heaven on earth (literally). One of the leaders is Reverend Geoffrey Wilson, who is hoping to find the true site of the Garden of Eden. Through letters and journals, he communicates his feelings and ideas as </span>
  <span id="freeText6244069700840266583" style="display:none">This narrative is truly a historical adventure. The reader can feel the salt of the sea wind throughout the whole story along the humorous and dramatic moments lived by an expedition full of colourful characters.<br /><br />It is the year 1857 and a voyage is leaving to Tasmania as it is believed to be heaven on earth (literally). One of the leaders is Reverend Geoffrey Wilson, who is hoping to find the true site of the Garden of Eden. Through letters and journals, he communicates his feelings and ideas as he faces the perils of the journey and the increasing discontent among the crew.<br /><br />An antagonist of the reverend is the racial-theorist Dr Potter, whose logical thinking and scientific beliefs show the reader the ideas of the time regarding the aboriginal people and the exotic flora and fauna he is wishing to encounter.<br /><br />Through the story, the reader is presented with the retellings of the experiences of different characters, like the captain of the ship, Captain Illiam Quillian Kewly, who keeps a secret from everybody: his ship is fleeing British Customs as it is a smuggling vessel. Another interesting point of view is Peevay's, an aboriginal young boy, member of a Tasmanian tribe unsuccessfully fighting the colonisers, their religious influences and the social customes they are trying to enforce in the local inhabitans.<br /><br />Matthew Kneale uses irony and sarcasm to deal with the less likeable characters and moments. The explotation of the natural resources and the cruelty used with the aborigines are portrayed as the consequence of the stupidity of those who believed they were doing their best to bring civilisation to the corners of the world.<br /><br />Nowadays, we are aware of the atrocities committed in the name of progress; however, surprises are waiting ahead for those who deserve being punished for their actions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6244069700840266583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_72998933" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72998933" />
    <a title="Usha Alexander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2790653-usha-alexander"><img alt="Usha Alexander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523978209p2/2790653.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72998933?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Usha Alexander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Usha Alexander" href="/user/show/2790653-usha-alexander">Usha Alexander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72998933" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5665322356284238076">This is one of the best books I've read in ages: resonant, entertaining, and affecting. At the first level of craft, Kneale's ability to make distinctive and authentic nearly every one of it's full chorus of voices is masterful. But it's more than that. The book's narrative follows two separate trajectories which intersect only briefly, towards the end of the book, and then again diverge; they could well have been two separate books. However, telling us these two stories in parallel brings us di</span>
  <span id="freeText5665322356284238076" style="display:none">This is one of the best books I've read in ages: resonant, entertaining, and affecting. At the first level of craft, Kneale's ability to make distinctive and authentic nearly every one of it's full chorus of voices is masterful. But it's more than that. The book's narrative follows two separate trajectories which intersect only briefly, towards the end of the book, and then again diverge; they could well have been two separate books. However, telling us these two stories in parallel brings us different insights into the characters, situations, and social reality of the times, as one story illuminates the other. Also, one story provides a certain amount of dark comedy, leavening what would be an otherwise entirely bleak tale.<br /><br />The opening narrative occurs in the years 1857 and 1858, concerning 3 amusingly mismatched Englishmen and their unhappy crew from the Isle of Man who are bound for the furthest shores of wild and unexplored Tasmania, there to prove it was home to the Garden of Eden. This tale is fully farcical and its characters are caricatures, which serves not only to bring in quite an element of comedy, but also meaningfully to dramatize certain strains of prevalent, Victorian-era social thought, namely, ideas of social progress and English superiority. The Reverend Wilson and Dr. Potter hilariously give life to these cultural assumptions, which were the impetus of colonialism, and demonstrate how they were entwined within both the religion and science of the day. Though some may look at these fictional characters and write them off as absurd, extreme, or sinister, they are, in fact, nothing of the kind; they are, rather, merely representative of many respectable thinkers of their day, who gave voice to, rationalized, and tried to systematize their cultural assumptions, doing the important work of explaining away the rapacity of the British empire, exactly as it was done by very real people in very real history. Meanwhile, the priceless Captain Kewley of amusingly bad judgment, both propels the long narrative through its comedy of errors and also provides a certain moral ballast, as one of the book's few reliable narrators.<br /><br />By contrast, the parallel narrative sticks closer to the ground, concerning itself with the experiences of settlers and aborigines of Tasmania, from 1820 through 1858. Some of the settlers are convicts, others are business people and farmers who came to make a new life or big profits in a new land. Through their many voices, we get very human glimpses into how people--even those who mean well--end up participating in, often even condoning, sometimes barbaric realities. What we see is as much savagery or humanity among the colonizers as among the colonized: Those few English--such as Dr. Pierce or Renshaw--who might truly recognize the humanity of the aborigines are sidelined, considered trouble-making extremists, while the more typical folk, wholly unconscious of the cultural assumptions and ideologies which guide their attitudes and actions (those same assumptions which are ultimately, in plain ugliness, epitomized by Rev. Wilson and Dr. Potter), inflict damage with their good intentions; meanwhile, those who really don't care at all, are openly barbaric. And around them all, supported by the same set of assumptions, the English penal system promotes its own flavor of inhumanity in the name of progress. It's a very human portrait of the times. While most of the settlers' voices make cameo appearances, the most constant character here is the tragic Peevay, unloved son of an intelligent, viciously angry aboriginal mother and a rapist father. It is through Peevay's intelligent, moderately self-reflective voice--a colorfully applied mish-mash of second-hand English learned from soldiers and preachers--that we see the destruction of a people and their way of life.<br /><br />Part way through the novel you begin to realize that by the time these two narratives intersect, it can only be after Peevay is a full-grown man (if he even survives) and his people are already destroyed. But when it finally happens, we have a different understanding of Peevay than we otherwise might have had, so that we may be less inclined to leap to easy judgments. As good Captain Kewley--blithely unaware of the darker matters narrated within this book--asks us in the opening paragraph:<br /><blockquote>Say a man catches a bullet through his skull in somebody's war, so where's the beginning of that? You might say that's easy. That little moment has its start the day our hero goes marching off to fight with his new soldier friends, all clever and smirking and waving at the girls. But does it, though? Why not the moment he first takes the schilling, his mouth hanging wide open like a harvest frog as he listens to the sergeant's flatterings? Or how about that bright sunny morning when he's just turned six and sees soldiers striding down the street, fierce and jangling? But then why not go right back, all the way, to that long, still night when a little baby is born, staring and new, with tiniest little hands? Hands you'd never think would grow strong enough one day to lift a heavy gun, and put a bullet through our poor dead friend's brain.</blockquote><br />And so as readers, we are challenged: Where does a story really begin? And, beneath the face of it, whose story is it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="5665322356284238076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10430699435899009343">I'm not sure how qualified I am to review a book beyond saying that I really liked it, adding perhaps a comparative, like, "I really liked it more than the great majority of books I've read in my life."<br/><br/>A wonderful story; well-defined characters you want to follow for a long time, a plot that moves along at a good pace and insight into a period of history and part of the world I knew little about. Not being able to predict the ending is a plus, though it's not a book where you're reading just to </span>
  <span id="freeText10430699435899009343" style="display:none">I'm not sure how qualified I am to review a book beyond saying that I really liked it, adding perhaps a comparative, like, "I really liked it more than the great majority of books I've read in my life."<br /><br />A wonderful story; well-defined characters you want to follow for a long time, a plot that moves along at a good pace and insight into a period of history and part of the world I knew little about. Not being able to predict the ending is a plus, though it's not a book where you're reading just to find out what happens. In fact, I found it similar, in a way, to the great Czech novel, The Good Soldier Schweik, in that I simply didn't want it to end; I just wanted to keep following adventures.<br /><br />I've been a big fan of books with anti-heroes for a long time and the central character, or at least the brightest star in the the cluster of central charcters, Captain Illiam Quillian Kewley, is just such a character. Bad enough to be running contraband into England but good hearted enough to bring a host of problems upon his crew and his boat as he transports his unusual passengers on a trip to Tazmania in search of, of all things, the Garden of Eden.<br /><br />The story is told as a series of journal entries by the Captain, his three passengers, an aborigine and assorted settlers of New Zealand, Tazmania and the surrounding islands. The format allows him to switch perspectives and even time periods, keeping the reader involved and interested in how the characters perceive shared events.<br /><br />Anyway, it was big, it was fun, it was very well written. I can't imagine I'll give a higher recommendation.<br /><br />Kneale manages to weave in a recounting of the devestation of the indigineous people of the islands</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer5625067663941412284">English Passengers by Matthew Kneale is a fast moving, incredible tale of a journey made by Manx smugglers who are forced to take boorish English passengers on an expedition to find the Garden of Eden in Tasmania. Each turn of the journey is characterized by bad luck, creative cursing and general head-butting on all accounts. The book features a different viewpoint every 5-10 pages,give or take, and you may find yourself cheating the cliffhangers to find out if a viewpoint you enjoy has met an u</span>
  <span id="freeText5625067663941412284" style="display:none">English Passengers by Matthew Kneale is a fast moving, incredible tale of a journey made by Manx smugglers who are forced to take boorish English passengers on an expedition to find the Garden of Eden in Tasmania. Each turn of the journey is characterized by bad luck, creative cursing and general head-butting on all accounts. The book features a different viewpoint every 5-10 pages,give or take, and you may find yourself cheating the cliffhangers to find out if a viewpoint you enjoy has met an untimely death or will resurface. <br /><br />Capt. Kewley, leader of the smugglers, is the most enjoyable sarcastic character I've read lately- even more enjoyable with the first person narrative. Also very moving is the account of Peevay- a Tasmanian native- as the world he knows comes to an end. This book, from beginning to end, is the most satisfying read I've had for ages.<br /><br />I edited this to add, this the only book I literally re-read three times in a row. Not because I needed to, but because the writing is so pleasurable and I start to miss the characters if I don't check up on them now and then. The only other book that I have also re-read as many times (although not in a row, more like once a year) is Larry McMurtry's Lonesome Dove. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

    <a title="Kristen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1041137-kristen"><img alt="Kristen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523196519p2/1041137.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/213776977?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristen" href="/user/show/1041137-kristen">Kristen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213776977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5515577754273857341">Lots of glowing reviews from Goodreads friends got me excited to read English Passengers, and it didn't disappoint. This novel about an ill-fated expedition to search for the Garden of Eden in Tasmania takes the voice of many narrators, some contributing only a single chapter and a core handful returning several times. The characters we get to know best include a few great ones: Illiam Quillian Kewley is a Manx smuggler with a wry sense of humor and a philosophical attitude toward the many misha</span>
  <span id="freeText5515577754273857341" style="display:none">Lots of glowing reviews from Goodreads friends got me excited to read English Passengers, and it didn't disappoint. This novel about an ill-fated expedition to search for the Garden of Eden in Tasmania takes the voice of many narrators, some contributing only a single chapter and a core handful returning several times. The characters we get to know best include a few great ones: Illiam Quillian Kewley is a Manx smuggler with a wry sense of humor and a philosophical attitude toward the many mishaps that befall him and his crew. Peevay, a Tasmanian aboriginal who has learned the language and customs of his colonizers, speaks a odd and wonderfully expressive brand of 19th Century English. Kneale also has a Dickens-like ability to create characters who embody some of humanity's most obnoxious traits, like the falsely modest vicar or the racist doctor. While these characters can be infuriating, they illustrate well the attitudes that caused Europeans to destroy native peoples -- without the novel becoming preachy or overbearing. The plotting here is excellent, too, as Kneale sets up several storylines that are each intriguing in their own right and meet up ingeniously for a satisfying ending.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5515577754273857341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_563787828" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/563787828" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730330-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294641149p2/730330.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/563787828?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/730330-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer563787828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10605331046887219866">The multiple-voice first-person narrative is difficult to pull off; shifting perspectives can destroy momentum, at the very least breaking up the rhythm. And if there aren't distinctive voices, the reader is left wondering: What was the point of this approach?<br/><br/>Matthew Kneale's "English Passengers" does have a bit of the herky-jerky to it, but, if you're going to take the narrative approach he does — if memory serves, there are more than 20 different narrators — it's hard to imagine it being done </span>
  <span id="freeText10605331046887219866" style="display:none">The multiple-voice first-person narrative is difficult to pull off; shifting perspectives can destroy momentum, at the very least breaking up the rhythm. And if there aren't distinctive voices, the reader is left wondering: What was the point of this approach?<br /><br />Matthew Kneale's "English Passengers" does have a bit of the herky-jerky to it, but, if you're going to take the narrative approach he does — if memory serves, there are more than 20 different narrators — it's hard to imagine it being done better (4.5 stars).<br /><br />This wonderfully written, sometimes challenging 19th-century tale features the folly of a man of God's search for the Garden of Eden in Tasmania, rum smugglers/pirates from the Isle of Man, British colonization of Tasmania and the wiping out of the culture of aboriginals.<br /><br />Kneale has written more than just a nautical adventure/exotic quest/native culture novel here. He weaves humor, pathos, adventure and character study into a highly ambitious and entertaining whole. The voices that tell this tale are original and compelling. Though he doesn't sustain greatness the entire way through — here and there are sections that bog down a bit — all in all, this is an extremely worthwhile and memorable tale.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10605331046887219866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385497435" href="/book/show/1276909.English_Passengers">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those interested in Australia, or getting read to travel Down Under.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/849756-leland?shelf=australia">australia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/849756-leland?shelf=seafaring">seafaring</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/849756-leland?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/849756-leland?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46363011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8002625357041701490">Wonderful reading! A very complex novel, told from many points of view. Characters, their language, experience, and perspective all weave together to portray the voyage of a Manx ship to Van Diemen's Land (Tasmania) to find Geological evidence of the Garden of Eden. Some of the most vivid descriptions of Tasmania I've ever read (and I've traveled the entire island quite thoroughly). From the prisoners of Port Arthur to the farms on the plain near Launceston, Kneale really captures the rugged, bi</span>
  <span id="freeText8002625357041701490" style="display:none">Wonderful reading! A very complex novel, told from many points of view. Characters, their language, experience, and perspective all weave together to portray the voyage of a Manx ship to Van Diemen's Land (Tasmania) to find Geological evidence of the Garden of Eden. Some of the most vivid descriptions of Tasmania I've ever read (and I've traveled the entire island quite thoroughly). From the prisoners of Port Arthur to the farms on the plain near Launceston, Kneale really captures the rugged, bizarre and captivating aspects of the land. One of the main narrators in the story is the Tasmanian Aboriginal Peevay who is one of the most Faulkneresque characters I've encountered. A wonderful, thought-provoking book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8002625357041701490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1024413348" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4987557934259851232">A true, unforgettable wonder. A literary masterpiece with a great historic touch that brings the beginning of 19th century back to life. The age of collonialism, of conquer and dominance. Of great fight between faith and science. Of death and sorrow of smaller nations, tribes that should never had been discovered. Here is a sad story of Tasmania and its last call for independance. Mathhew Kenale's novel is rich, moving with many voices to hear.</span>

          </span>

    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2y4keSYJp9Hy8BV9mppa9GFsws+QrVb5KJY+4HBP+KW+kdjAgWVgk8RA1zoNutCtcBu1MpRAK2S8eZ+8u45ZuQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UgYi6FSLM32mBviYOXLHC2p40U37jvFpa4CZV4XKWaw3ud5R8+f0P5C2Ot+uUk1Sew+msP9jjPT/bzgLTgv4sA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_268016'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/268016.The_Hiding_Place?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Hiding Place<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/156396.Trezza_Azzopardi?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Trezza Azzopardi<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.54 avg rating &mdash; 1,477 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2000\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer10947634980562327203\">A finalist for the prestigious Man Booker Prize, The Hiding Place -- Welsh novelist Trezza Azzopardi\'s lyrical tale of an immigrant family in Cardiff -- has been compared to Frank McCourt\'s bleak, stirring memoir Angela\'s Ashes. But The Hiding Place <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10947634980562327203\" style=\"display:none\">A finalist for the prestigious Man Booker Prize, The Hiding Place -- Welsh novelist Trezza Azzopardi\'s lyrical tale of an immigrant family in Cardiff -- has been compared to Frank McCourt\'s bleak, stirring memoir Angela\'s Ashes. But The Hiding Place need not &quot;hide&quot; behind any ready-made comparisons; Azzopardi\'s astonishing, tension-filled debut stands assuredly on its own as a work of tremendous power and originality. \n\nThe Hiding Place is narrated by Dolores, the youngest of six daughters born to a Maltese immigrant father and a Welsh mother. With one hand permanently disfigured by a fire when she was only one month old -- the hand is beautifully described by the author as &quot;a closed white tulip standing in the rain; a cutoff creamy marble in the shape of a Saint; a church candle with its tears flowing down the bulb of wrist&quot; -- Dolores has always been treated as an outcast. Her father, Frankie Gauci, is an incorrigible gambler who bets &quot;more than he can afford to lose.&quot; On the day Dolores is born, he loses his half-share of a caf&amp;eacute, as well as the apartment above it where his family lives. Everything in Frankie\'s life is potential currency, including his family; he even sells his second-oldest daughter Marina to gangster Joe Medora in exchange for a house and money to pay off his debts. Dolores\'s mother, Mary, is driven to the edge of insanity as she watches the world around her collapse, helpless to save even her children from her husband\'s vices. \n\nAt times, The Hiding Place paints a phantasmagoric portrait of cruelty, but Trezza Azzopardi\'s gracefully exacting prose saves her tale from becoming a shock-fest of the sort you would expect on daytime television talk shows. Azzopardi forges profundity through delicately interwoven double-sided images: rabbits that are the children\'s playthings, until they are brutally slaughtered by their father; trunks, rooms, and cages that can either protect or ensnare; and most abundantly and most significantly, fire, which can warm as well as ravage. Even Dolores\'s older sister Fran is sent away to a home for being a pyromaniac, craving risk like her father, &quot;gambling on how hot, how high, on how long she can bear it.&quot; \n\nWhile some readers may wonder how Dolores is able to relate events that happened when she was so young, it is easy to associate these stories with the phantom pains she feels in her missing fingers, her ability to &quot;miss something [she] never knew.&quot; The story comes to us in a dreamlike tapestry, weaving together different times and perspectives. Consequently, the narrative is fragmented, leaving the reader with half-tellings, missing details, stories that unfold only in the retelling, and a sense that the only fact we can be certain of is the profound meaning she imparts through them. The Hiding Place is as much a portrait of a family\'s destruction as it is an exploration of how memory bends and buckles under the weight of ruin, and how &quot;blame can be twisted like a flame in draught; it will burn and burn.&quot;<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10947634980562327203\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div Only Churchill refuses to listen.At the center of this high-stakes game of deals and doubledealing is Willard Mayer, an OSS operative who has been chosen by FDR to serve as his envoy. He is the perfect foil for the steamy world of deception, betrayals, and assassinations that make up the moral universe of realpolitik. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3485048587199253693">Matthew Kneale was born in London in 1960, read Modern History at Oxford University and on graduating in 1982, spent a year teaching English in Japan, where he began writing short stories. <br/><br/>Kneale is the son of writers <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/491508.Nigel_Kneale' title='Nigel Kneale' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nigel Kneale</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/30703.Judith_Kerr' title='Judith Kerr' rel='nofollow noopener'>Judith Kerr</a>, and the grandson of essayist and theatre critic <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/707248.Alfred_Kerr' title='Alfred Kerr' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alfred Kerr</a>.</span>
  <span id="freeText3485048587199253693" style="display:none">Matthew Kneale was born in London in 1960, read Modern History at Oxford University and on graduating in 1982, spent a year teaching English in Japan, where he began writing short stories. <br /><br />Kneale is the son of writers <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/491508.Nigel_Kneale" title="Nigel Kneale" rel="nofollow noopener">Nigel Kneale</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/30703.Judith_Kerr" title="Judith Kerr" rel="nofollow noopener">Judith Kerr</a>, and the grandson of essayist and theatre critic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/707248.Alfred_Kerr" title="Alfred Kerr" rel="nofollow noopener">Alfred Kerr</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3485048587199253693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1805006-english-passengers">Quotes from English Passengers</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;My opponents' first argument was that the rocks of the earth--which are generally agreed to have once been in a hot and melted state--would have required far longer to lose their heat than the Scriptures described. My reply was that the earth had indeed cooled at great speed, being made possible by a process I termed Divine Refrigeration.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/7210773-my-opponents-first-argument-was-that-the-rocks-of-the">1 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;All at once I felt myself haunted by a terrible vision, of a world without guidance: a land of emptiness, where all was ruled by the madness of chance. How could one endure such a place, where all significance was lost? I myself would mean nothing, but would merely be a kind of self-invention: a speck upon the wind, calling itself Wilson. I felt my spirit waver, as if it were toppling into the abyss before me.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
